class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject mw-editable page-Moons_of_Jupiter rootpage-Moons_of_Jupiter skin-vector action-view"> <div class="noprint" CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div class="mw-indicator" id="mw-indicator-featured-star"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Featured_lists" title="This is a featured list. Click here for more information."><img alt="This is a featured list. Click here for more information." data-file-height="438" data-file-width="462" decoding="async" height="19" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/20px-Cscr-featured.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/30px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/40px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Moons of Jupiter</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the moons of Jupiter. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter_(disambiguation)" title="Moons of Jupiter (disambiguation)">Moons of Jupiter (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The natural satellites of the planet Jupiter</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_family.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1654" data-file-width="2081" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Jupiter_family.jpg/250px-Jupiter_family.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Jupiter_family.jpg/375px-Jupiter_family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Jupiter_family.jpg/500px-Jupiter_family.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_family.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A montage of Jupiter and its four largest moons (distance and sizes not to scale)</div></div></div><p>\nThere are 79 known <b>moons of Jupiter</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shep-main_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-shep-main-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vox-10-new-jupitermoons_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-vox-10-new-jupitermoons-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carnegie_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-carnegie-3">[3]</a></sup> This gives <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> the largest number of <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> with reasonably stable orbits of any planet in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4">[4]</a></sup> The most massive of the moons are the four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>, which were independently discovered in 1610 by <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> and were <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_discovery_of_Solar_System_planets_and_their_moons" title="Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons">the first objects</a> found to orbit a body that was neither Earth nor the Sun. From the end of the 19th century, dozens of much smaller Jovian moons have been discovered and have received the names of lovers or daughters of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman god</a> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a> or his <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek equivalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>. The Galilean moons are by far the largest and most massive objects to orbit Jupiter, with the remaining 75 known moons and <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Jupiter" title="Rings of Jupiter">the rings</a> together comprising just 0.003% of the total orbiting mass.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif/220px-Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif/330px-Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif/440px-Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_and_the_Galilean_moons_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The orbit and motion of the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> around Jupiter, as captured by <a href="/wiki/JunoCam" title="JunoCam">JunoCam</a> aboard the <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)"><i>Juno</i> spacecraft</a>.</div></div></div><p>Of Jupiter\'s moons, eight are <a href="/wiki/Regular_moon" title="Regular moon">regular satellites</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_motion" title="Direct motion">prograde</a> and nearly circular orbits that are not greatly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclined</a> with respect to Jupiter\'s equatorial plane. The Galilean satellites are nearly spherical in shape due to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planemo" title="Planemo">planetary mass</a>, and so would be considered at least <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planets</a> if they were in direct orbit around the Sun. The other four regular satellites are much smaller and closer to Jupiter; these serve as sources of the dust that makes up Jupiter\'s rings. The remainder of Jupiter\'s moons are <a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">irregular satellites</a> whose prograde and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbits</a> are much farther from Jupiter and have high inclinations and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricities</a>. These moons were probably captured by Jupiter from solar orbits. Twenty-seven of the irregular satellites have not yet been officially named.\n</p><div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Characteristics"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Characteristics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Origin_and_evolution"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Origin and evolution</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Discovery"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Discovery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Naming"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Naming</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Groups"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Groups</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Regular_satellites"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Regular satellites</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Irregular_satellites"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Irregular satellites</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#List"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">List</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Exploration"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Exploration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The physical and orbital characteristics of the moons vary widely. The four Galileans are all over 3,100 kilometres (1,900&#160;mi) in diameter; the largest Galilean, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, is the ninth <a href="/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size" title="List of Solar System objects by size">largest object</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, after the Sun and seven of the <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>, Ganymede being larger than <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>. All other Jovian moons are less than 250 kilometres (160&#160;mi) in diameter, with most barely exceeding 5 kilometres (3.1&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[note 1]</a></sup> Their <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbital shapes</a> range from nearly perfectly circular to highly <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclined</a>, and many revolve in the direction opposite to Jupiter\'s spin (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde motion</a>). <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">Orbital periods</a> range from seven hours (taking less time than Jupiter does to spin around its axis), to some three thousand times more (almost three Earth years).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">Origin and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1435" data-file-width="1773" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png/290px-Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png/435px-Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png/580px-Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relative_Masses_of_Jovian_Satellites.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The relative masses of the Jovian moons. Those smaller than Europa are not visible at this scale, and combined would only be visible at 100&#215; magnification.</div></div></div><p>Jupiter\'s regular satellites are believed to have formed from a circumplanetary disk, a ring of accreting gas and solid debris analogous to a <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2009_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canup2009-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alibert2005_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alibert2005-7">[6]</a></sup> They may be the remnants of a score of Galilean-mass satellites that formed early in Jupiter\'s history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2009_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canup2009-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsci_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-newsci-8">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Simulations suggest that, while the disk had a relatively high mass at any given moment, over time a substantial fraction (several tenths of a percent) of the mass of Jupiter captured from the solar nebula was passed through it. However, only 2% of the proto-disk mass of Jupiter is required to explain the existing satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2009_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Canup2009-6">[5]</a></sup> Thus there may have been several generations of Galilean-mass satellites in Jupiter\'s early history. Each generation of moons might have spiraled into Jupiter, because of drag from the disk, with new moons then forming from the new debris captured from the solar nebula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2009_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Canup2009-6">[5]</a></sup> By the time the present (possibly fifth) generation formed, the disk had thinned so that it no longer greatly interfered with the moons\' orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newsci_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-newsci-8">[7]</a></sup> The current Galilean moons were still affected, falling into and being partially protected by an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with each other, which still exists for Io, Europa, and Ganymede. Ganymede\'s larger mass means that it would have migrated inward at a faster rate than Europa or Io.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2009_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Canup2009-6">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The outer, irregular moons are thought to have originated from captured <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>, whereas the protolunar disk was still massive enough to absorb much of their momentum and thus capture them into orbit. Many are believed to have broken up by mechanical stresses during capture, or afterward by collisions with other small bodies, producing the moons we see today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-9">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:327px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Galilean_satellites_(the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter).tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1830" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif/lossy-page1-325px-The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif/lossy-page1-488px-The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif/lossy-page1-650px-The_Galilean_satellites_%28the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter%29.tif.jpg 2x" width="325"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Galilean_satellites_(the_four_largest_moons_of_Jupiter).tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Galilean moons. From left to right, in order of increasing distance from Jupiter: <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>; <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>; <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_discovery_of_Solar_System_planets_and_their_moons" title="Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons">Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons</a></div>\n<p>Some scholars propose that the earliest record of a Jovian moon (Ganymede or Callisto) is a note by Chinese astronomer <a href="/wiki/Gan_De" title="Gan De">Gan De</a> of an observation around 364 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the first certain observations of Jupiter\'s satellites were those of <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> in 1609.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galileo89_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galileo89-11">[10]</a></sup> By January 1610, he had sighted the four massive <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> with his 30&#215; <a href="/wiki/Magnification" title="Magnification">magnification</a> <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, and he published his results in March 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Simon Marius had independently discovered the moons one day after Galileo, although he did not publish his book on the subject until 1614. Even so, the names Marius assigned are used today: <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>; <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>; <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> No additional satellites were discovered until <a href="/wiki/Edward_Emerson_Barnard" title="Edward Emerson Barnard">E. E. Barnard</a> observed <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a> in 1892.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnard1892_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnard1892-14">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nWith the aid of telescopic photography, further discoveries followed quickly over the course of the <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">20th century</a>. <a href="/wiki/Himalia_(moon)" title="Himalia (moon)">Himalia</a> was discovered in 1904,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Elara_(moon)" title="Elara (moon)">Elara</a> in 1905,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perrine_1905_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perrine_1905-16">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pasiphae_(moon)" title="Pasiphae (moon)">Pasiphae</a> in 1908,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melotte_1908_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melotte_1908-17">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sinope_(moon)" title="Sinope (moon)">Sinope</a> in 1914,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lysithea_(moon)" title="Lysithea (moon)">Lysithea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carme_(moon)" title="Carme (moon)">Carme</a> in 1938,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_1938_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson_1938-19">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ananke_(moon)" title="Ananke (moon)">Ananke</a> in 1951,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicolson1951_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolson1951-20">[19]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Leda_(moon)" title="Leda (moon)">Leda</a> in 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kowal_1974_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kowal_1974-21">[20]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter-moons.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="508" data-file-width="887" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Jupiter-moons.jpg/250px-Jupiter-moons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Jupiter-moons.jpg/375px-Jupiter-moons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Jupiter-moons.jpg/500px-Jupiter-moons.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter-moons.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter and the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> through a 25&#160;cm (10&#160;in) <a href="/wiki/Meade_LX200" title="Meade LX200">Meade LX200</a> telescope.</div></div></div><p>By the time that the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager space probes</a> reached Jupiter, around 1979, 13 moons had been discovered, not including <a href="/wiki/Themisto_(moon)" title="Themisto (moon)">Themisto</a>, which had been observed in 1975,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> but was lost until 2000 due to insufficient initial observation data. The Voyager spacecraft discovered an additional three inner moons in 1979: <a href="/wiki/Metis_(moon)" title="Metis (moon)">Metis</a>; <a href="/wiki/Adrastea_(moon)" title="Adrastea (moon)">Adrastea</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Thebe_(moon)" title="Thebe (moon)">Thebe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Synnott1980_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Synnott1980-23">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>No additional moons were discovered for two decades, generally during the 1980s and 1990s, but between October 1999 and February 2003, researchers found another 34 moons using sensitive ground-based detectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovered_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-discovered-24">[23]</a></sup> These are tiny moons, in long, <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a>, generally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbits</a>, and averaging 3&#160;km (1.9&#160;mi) in diameter, with the largest being just 9&#160;km (5.6&#160;mi) across. All of these moons are thought to have been captured <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroidal</a> or perhaps <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a> bodies, possibly fragmented into several pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irreg_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-irreg-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 2015, a total of 15 additional moons were discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[25]</a></sup> Two more were discovered in 2016 by the team led by <a href="/wiki/Scott_S._Sheppard" title="Scott S. Sheppard">Scott S. Sheppard</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Institution_for_Science" title="Carnegie Institution for Science">Carnegie Institution for Science</a>, bringing the total to 69.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> On 17 July 2018, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> confirmed that Sheppard\'s team discovered ten more moons around Jupiter, bringing the total number to 79.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Among these is <a href="/wiki/Valetudo_(moon)" title="Valetudo (moon)">Valetudo</a>, which has a <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">prograde</a> orbit, but crosses paths with several moons that have <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">retrograde</a> orbits, making an eventual collision&#8212;at some point on a billions of years timescale&#8212;likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Additional <a href="/wiki/Moonlet" title="Moonlet">tiny moons</a> likely exist but remain undiscovered, as they are very difficult for astronomers to detect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Outer_planet_moons.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="824" data-file-width="1471" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Outer_planet_moons.png/220px-Outer_planet_moons.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Outer_planet_moons.png/330px-Outer_planet_moons.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Outer_planet_moons.png/440px-Outer_planet_moons.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Outer_planet_moons.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The number of moons known for each of the four outer planets up to July 2018. Jupiter currently has 79 known satellites.</div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Naming_of_moons" title="Naming of moons">Naming of moons</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileans.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="184" data-file-width="184" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Galileans.svg/220px-Galileans.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Galileans.svg/330px-Galileans.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Galileans.svg/440px-Galileans.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileans.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Galilean moons and their orbits around Jupiter.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Galilean moons of Jupiter (<a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>) were named by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> soon after their discovery in 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marazzini_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marazzini-31">[30]</a></sup> However, these names fell out of favor until the 20th century. The astronomical literature instead simply referred to "Jupiter I", "Jupiter II", etc., or "the first satellite of Jupiter", "Jupiter\'s second satellite", and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marazzini_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marazzini-31">[30]</a></sup> The names Io, Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto became popular in the mid-20th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marazzini_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-marazzini-32">[31]</a></sup> whereas the rest of the moons remained unnamed and were usually numbered in Roman numerals V (5) to XII (12).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (September 2018)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Jupiter V was discovered in 1892 and given the name <i><a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a></i> by a popular though unofficial convention, a name first used by French astronomer <a href="/wiki/Camille_Flammarion" title="Camille Flammarion">Camille Flammarion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovered_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-discovered-24">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The other moons were simply labeled by their Roman numeral (e.g. Jupiter IX) in the majority of astronomical literature until the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaposchkin_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaposchkin-34">[33]</a></sup> In 1975, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>\'s (IAU) Task Group for Outer Solar System Nomenclature granted names to satellites V&#8211;XIII,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau75_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-iau75-35">[34]</a></sup> and provided for a formal naming process for future satellites still to be discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau75_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-iau75-35">[34]</a></sup> The practice was to name newly discovered moons of Jupiter after lovers and favorites of the god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_(god)" title="Jupiter (god)">Jupiter</a> (<a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>) and, since 2004, also after their descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-iau-36">[35]</a></sup> All of Jupiter\'s satellites from XXXIV (<a href="/wiki/Euporie_(moon)" title="Euporie (moon)">Euporie</a>) onward are named after daughters of Jupiter or Zeus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-iau-36">[35]</a></sup> except LIII (<a href="/wiki/Dia_(moon)" title="Dia (moon)">Dia</a>), named after a lover of Jupiter, and LXII (<a href="/wiki/Valetudo_(moon)" title="Valetudo (moon)">Valetudo</a>), named after a great-granddaughter of Jupiter. Names ending with "a" or "o" are used for prograde irregular satellites (the latter for highly inclined satellites), and names ending with "e" are used for retrograde irregulars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> The most recently confirmed moons Jupiter LI through LXXII (with the exception of Jupiter LIII Dia and Jupiter LXII Valetudo) have not received names.\n</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> share <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Name_conflicts_of_solar_system_objects" title="Name conflicts of solar system objects">the same names</a> as moons of Jupiter: <a href="/wiki/9_Metis" title="9 Metis">9 Metis</a>, <a href="/wiki/38_Leda" title="38 Leda">38 Leda</a>, <a href="/wiki/52_Europa" title="52 Europa">52 Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/85_Io" title="85 Io">85 Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/113_Amalthea" title="113 Amalthea">113 Amalthea</a>, <a href="/wiki/239_Adrastea" title="239 Adrastea">239 Adrastea</a>. Two more asteroids previously shared the names of Jovian moons until spelling differences were made permanent by the IAU: <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> and asteroid <a href="/wiki/1036_Ganymed" title="1036 Ganymed">1036 Ganymed</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a> and asteroid <a href="/wiki/204_Kallisto" title="204 Kallisto">204 Kallisto</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Groups">Groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg/300px-TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg/450px-TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg/600px-TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheIrregulars_JUPITER.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The orbits of Jupiter\'s irregular satellites, and how they cluster into groups: by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a> (the horizontal axis in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigametre" title="Gigametre">Gm</a>); by <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">orbital inclination</a> (the vertical axis); and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> (the yellow lines). The relative sizes are indicated by the  circles.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regular_satellites">Regular satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Regular satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>These have prograde and nearly circular orbits of low inclination and are split into two groups:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><ul><li><b>Inner satellites</b> or <b>Amalthea group</b>: <a href="/wiki/Metis_(moon)" title="Metis (moon)">Metis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adrastea_(moon)" title="Adrastea (moon)">Adrastea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(moon)" title="Amalthea (moon)">Amalthea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thebe_(moon)" title="Thebe (moon)">Thebe</a>. These orbit very close to Jupiter; the innermost two orbit in less than a Jovian day.  The latter two are respectively the fifth and seventh largest moons in the  Jovian system. Observations suggest that at least the largest member, Amalthea, did not form on its present orbit, but farther from the planet, or that it is a captured Solar System body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> These moons, along with a number of as-yet-unseen inner moonlets, replenish and maintain Jupiter\'s faint <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Jupiter" title="Rings of Jupiter">ring system</a>. Metis and Adrastea help to maintain Jupiter\'s main ring, whereas Amalthea and Thebe each maintain their own faint outer rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><ul><li><b>Main group</b> or <b><a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>. They are some of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_moons" title="List of moons">the largest objects</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> outside the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the eight planets in terms of mass and are larger than any known <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>. Ganymede exceeds even the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> in diameter. They are respectively the fourth-, sixth-, first-, and third-largest <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a> in the Solar System, containing approximately 99.997% of the total mass in orbit around Jupiter, while Jupiter is almost 5,000 times more massive than the Galilean moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Big4Mass_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Big4Mass-41">[note 2]</a></sup> The inner moons are in a 1:2:4 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a>. Models suggest that they formed by slow <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> in the low-density Jovian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">subnebula</a>&#8212;a disc of the gas and dust that existed around Jupiter after its formation&#8212;which lasted up to 10 million years in the case of Callisto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> Several are suspected of having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_oceans" title="Extraterrestrial oceans">subsurface oceans</a>.</li></ul></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Irregular_satellites">Irregular satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Irregular satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_moons_anim.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="459" data-file-width="549" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Jupiter_moons_anim.gif/300px-Jupiter_moons_anim.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Jupiter_moons_anim.gif/450px-Jupiter_moons_anim.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Jupiter_moons_anim.gif 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_moons_anim.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jupiter\'s outer moons and their highly inclined orbits</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_satellite" title="Irregular satellite">Irregular satellite</a></div>\n<p>The irregular satellites are substantially smaller objects with more distant and eccentric orbits. They form families with shared similarities in orbit (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a>) and composition; it is believed that these are at least partially <a href="/wiki/Collisional_family" title="Collisional family">collisional families</a> that were created when larger (but still small) parent bodies were shattered by impacts from asteroids captured by Jupiter\'s gravitational field. These families bear the names of their largest members. The identification of satellite families is tentative, but the following are typically listed:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shep_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-shep-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2003_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grav2003-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-list_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-list-45">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_motion" title="Direct motion">Prograde</a> satellites:</li></ul>\n<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Themisto_(moon)" title="Themisto (moon)">Themisto</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2003_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grav2003-44">[42]</a></sup> is the innermost irregular moon and is not part of a known family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shep_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-shep-43">[41]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><ul><li>The <b><a href="/wiki/Himalia_group" title="Himalia group">Himalia group</a></b> is spread over barely 1.4&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigameter" title="Gigameter">Gm</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a>, 1.6&#176; in inclination (27.5&#160;&#177;&#160;0.8&#176;), and <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricities</a> between 0.11 and 0.25. It has been suggested that the group could be a remnant of the break-up of an asteroid from the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2003_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grav2003-44">[42]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Carpo_(moon)" title="Carpo (moon)">Carpo</a> is another prograde moon and is not part of a known family. It has the highest inclination of all of the prograde moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shep_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-shep-43">[41]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Valetudo_(moon)" title="Valetudo (moon)">Valetudo</a>, reported 2018, is the outermost prograde moon and is not part of a known family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shep_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-shep-43">[41]</a></sup> It has a prograde orbit, but it crosses paths with several moons that have retrograde orbits and may in the future collide with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-46">[44]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="632" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png/300px-Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png/450px-Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png/600px-Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter_sats_i_vs_e.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Retrograde satellites: inclinations (&#176;) vs. eccentricities, with Carme\'s (orange) and Ananke\'s (yellow) groups identified. Data as of 2009.</div></div></div>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">Retrograde</a> satellites:</li></ul>\n<dl><dd><ul><li>The <b><a href="/wiki/Carme_group" title="Carme group">Carme group</a></b> is spread over only 1.2&#160;Gm in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axis</a>, 1.6&#176; in inclination (165.7&#160;&#177;&#160;0.8&#176;), and eccentricities between 0.23 and 0.27. It is very homogeneous in color (light red) and is believed to have originated from a <a href="/wiki/D-type_asteroid" title="D-type asteroid">D-type asteroid</a> progenitor, possibly a Jupiter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid#Trojan_asteroids" title="Trojan asteroid">Trojan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irreg_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-irreg-25">[24]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><ul><li>The <b><a href="/wiki/Ananke_group" title="Ananke group">Ananke group</a></b> has a relatively wider spread than the previous groups, over 2.4&#160;Gm in semi-major axis, 8.1&#176; in inclination (between 145.7&#176; and 154.8&#176;), and eccentricities between 0.02 and 0.28. Most of the members appear gray, and are believed to have formed from the breakup of a captured asteroid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irreg_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-irreg-25">[24]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><ul><li>The <b><a href="/wiki/Pasiphae_group" title="Pasiphae group">Pasiphae group</a></b> is quite dispersed, with a spread over 1.3&#160;Gm, inclinations between 144.5&#176; and 158.3&#176;, and eccentricities between 0.25 and 0.43.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irreg_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-irreg-25">[24]</a></sup> The colors also vary significantly, from red to grey, which might be the result of multiple collisions. <a href="/wiki/Sinope_(moon)" title="Sinope (moon)">Sinope</a>, sometimes included in the Pasiphae group,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irreg_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-irreg-25">[24]</a></sup> is red and, given the difference in inclination, it could have been captured independently;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grav2003_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Grav2003-44">[42]</a></sup> Pasiphae and Sinope are also trapped in <a href="/wiki/Secular_resonance" title="Secular resonance">secular resonances</a> with Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny2004_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny2004-47">[45]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List">List</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: List">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div style="float: right; width:20em;">\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin:0; font-size:90%">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="5" style="background:#eaeaea">Key\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td style="background:#ccf; width:19.9%;">&#9824;<br/> <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>\n</td>\n<td style="background:#efefef; width:19.9%;">&#8224;<br/> Prograde irregular moons\n</td>\n<td style="background:#d3d3d3; width:19.9%;">&#8225;<br/> Retrograde moons\n</td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>The moons of Jupiter are listed below by orbital period. Moons massive enough for their surfaces to have <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">collapsed</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroid</a> are highlighted in bold. These are the four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>, which are comparable in size to the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. The other moons are much smaller, with the least massive Galilean moon being more than 7000 times more massive than the most massive of the other moons. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_satellite" title="Irregular satellite">irregular</a> captured moons are shaded light gray when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_motion" title="Direct motion">prograde</a> and dark gray when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde</a>. All orbits are based on the estimated orbit on the <a href="/wiki/Julian_day" title="Julian day">Julian date</a> 2457000, or 3 September 2017. As several moons of Jupiter are currently lost, these orbital elements may be only rough approximations. As of 2018, seven satellites are considered to be lost. These are <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_2" title="S/2003 J 2">S/2003 J 2</a>, <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_4" title="S/2003 J 4">S/2003 J 4</a>, <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_9" title="S/2003 J 9">S/2003 J 9</a>, <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_10" title="S/2003 J 10">S/2003 J 10</a>, <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_12" title="S/2003 J 12">S/2003 J 12</a>, <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_16" title="S/2003 J 16">S/2003 J 16</a>, and <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_23" title="S/2003 J 23">S/2003 J 23</a>. A number of other moons have only been observed for a year or two, but have decent enough orbits to be easily measurable even in 2018.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)#Exploration" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto (moon) &#167;&#160;Exploration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)#Observational_history" title="Io (moon)">Io (moon) &#167;&#160;Observational history</a></div>\n<p>The first spacecraft to visit Jupiter were <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer&#160;10</a></i> in 1973, and <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer&#160;11</a></i> a year later, taking low-resolution images of the four <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[56]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager&#160;1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager&#160;2</a></i> probes visited Jupiter in 1979, discovering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanism_on_Io" title="Volcanism on Io">volcanic activity on&#160;Io</a> and the presence of water <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a> on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i> probe to Saturn flew by Jupiter in 2000 and collected data on interactions of the Galilean moons with Jupiter\'s extended atmosphere. The <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i> spacecraft flew by Jupiter in 2007 and made improved measurements of its satellites\' orbital parameters.\n</p><p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo_spacecraft" title="Galileo spacecraft">Galileo</a></i> spacecraft was the first to enter orbit around Jupiter, arriving in 1995 and studying it until 2003. During this period, <i>Galileo</i> gathered a large amount of information about the Jovian system, making close approaches to all of the Galilean moons and finding evidence for thin atmospheres on three of them, as well as the possibility of liquid water beneath the surfaces of Europa, Ganymede, and Callisto. It also discovered a <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)#Magnetosphere" title="Ganymede (moon)">magnetic field around Ganymede</a>.\n</p><p>In 2016, the <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i> spacecraft imaged the Galilean moons from above their orbital plane as it approached Jupiter orbit insertion, creating a time-lapse movie of their motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jupiter%27s_moons_in_fiction" title="Jupiter\'s moons in fiction">Jupiter\'s moons in fiction</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojan</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Jupiter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For comparison, the area of a sphere with diameter 250&#160;km is about the area of <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> and comparable to the area of <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>. The area of a sphere with diameter 5&#160;km is about the area of <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">Guernsey</a> and somewhat more than the area of <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">San Marino</a>. (But note that these smaller moons are not spherical.)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Big4Mass-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Big4Mass_41-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jupiter Mass of 1.8986&#160;&#215;&#160;10<sup>27</sup>&#160;kg / <a class="external text" href="http://ssd.jpl.nasa.gov/?sat_phys_par" rel="nofollow">Mass of Galilean moons</a> 3.93&#160;&#215;&#160;10<sup>23</sup>&#160;kg = 4,828</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Order refers to the position among other moons with respect to their average distance from Jupiter.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Label refers to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_numeral" title="Roman numeral">Roman numeral</a> attributed to each moon in order of their naming.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Diameters with multiple entries such as "60&#8201;&#215;&#8201;40&#8201;&#215;&#8201;34" reflect that the body is not a perfect <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroid</a> and that each of its dimensions have been measured well enough.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Periods with negative values are retrograde.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"?" refers to group assignments that are not considered sure yet.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
